### Newspapers

[
[edit](/w/index.php?title=Pittsburgh&action=edit&section=46 "Edit section: Newspapers")

]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/KDKAGateway.jpg/220px-KDKAGateway.jpg)](/wiki/File:KDKAGateway.jpg)


[KDKA-AM](/wiki/KDKA_(AM) "KDKA (AM)")
's studios at Gateway Center

There are two major daily newspapers in Pittsburgh: the
*[Pittsburgh Post-Gazette](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Post-Gazette "Pittsburgh Post-Gazette")*
and the
*[Pittsburgh Tribune-Review](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Tribune-Review "Pittsburgh Tribune-Review")*
online only (no longer in print for Pittsburgh Area). Weekly papers in the region include the
*[Pittsburgh Business Times](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Business_Times "Pittsburgh Business Times")*
,
*[Pittsburgh City Paper](/wiki/Pittsburgh_City_Paper "Pittsburgh City Paper")*
,
*[Pittsburgh Catholic](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Catholic "Pittsburgh Catholic")*
,
*[Pittsburgh Jewish Chronicle](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Jewish_Chronicle "Pittsburgh Jewish Chronicle")*
,
*The New People*
, and the
*[New Pittsburgh Courier](/wiki/New_Pittsburgh_Courier "New Pittsburgh Courier")*
. Independent student-written university-based newspapers include
*[The Pitt News](/wiki/The_Pitt_News "The Pitt News")*
of the
[University of Pittsburgh](/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh "University of Pittsburgh")
,
*[The Tartan](/wiki/The_Tartan_(Carnegie_Mellon_University) "The Tartan (Carnegie Mellon University)")*
of
[Carnegie Mellon University](/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University "Carnegie Mellon University")
,
*[The Duquesne Duke](/wiki/The_Duquesne_Duke "The Duquesne Duke")*
of
[Duquesne University](/wiki/Duquesne_University "Duquesne University")
, and
*[The Globe](/wiki/The_Globe_student_newspaper "The Globe student newspaper")*
of
[Point Park University](/wiki/Point_Park_University "Point Park University")
. The
[University of Pittsburgh School of Law](/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh_School_of_Law "University of Pittsburgh School of Law")
is also home to
[JURIST](/wiki/JURIST "JURIST")
, the world's only university-based legal news service.
[[
241
]](#cite_note-251)

### Television

[
[edit](/w/index.php?title=Pittsburgh&action=edit&section=47 "Edit section: Television")

]

The Pittsburgh metro area is served by multiple local television and radio stations. The Pittsburgh
[designated market area](/wiki/Designated_market_area "Designated market area")
(DMA) is the 22nd-largest in the U.S. with 1,163,150 homes (1.045% of the total U.S.).
[[
242
]](#cite_note-nielsen-252)
The major network television stations include
[KDKA-TV](/wiki/KDKA-TV "KDKA-TV")
2 (
[CBS](/wiki/CBS "CBS")
),
[WTAE](/wiki/WTAE-TV "WTAE-TV")
4 (
[ABC](/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company "American Broadcasting Company")
),
[WPXI](/wiki/WPXI "WPXI")
11 (
[NBC](/wiki/NBC "NBC")
),
[WINP-TV](/wiki/WINP-TV "WINP-TV")
16 (
[Ion](/wiki/Ion_Television "Ion Television")
),
[WPKD-TV](/wiki/WPKD-TV "WPKD-TV")
19 (
[Independent](/wiki/Independent_station "Independent station")
),
[WPNT](/wiki/WPNT "WPNT")
22 (
[The CW](/wiki/The_CW "The CW")
/
[MyNetworkTV](/wiki/MyNetworkTV "MyNetworkTV")
),
[WPCB](/wiki/WPCB "WPCB")
40 (
[Cornerstone](/wiki/Cornerstone_Television "Cornerstone Television")
), and
[WPGH-TV](/wiki/WPGH-TV "WPGH-TV")
53 (
[Fox](/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company "Fox Broadcasting Company")
). KDKA-TV, WINP-TV, and WPCB are owned-and-operated by their respective networks.

[WQED](/wiki/WQED_(TV) "WQED (TV)")
13 is the local
[PBS](/wiki/PBS "PBS")
member station in Pittsburgh. It was established on April 1, 1954, and was the first community-sponsored television station and the fifth public station in the United States. The station has produced much original content for PBS, including
*[Mr. Rogers' Neighborhood](/wiki/Mr._Rogers%27_Neighborhood "Mr. Rogers' Neighborhood")*
, several
[National Geographic](/wiki/National_Geographic_Society "National Geographic Society")
specials, and
*[Where in the World is Carmen Sandiego?](/wiki/Where_in_the_World_Is_Carmen_Sandiego%3F_(game_show) "Where in the World Is Carmen Sandiego? (game show)")*
[[
243
]](#cite_note-WQED-253)

### Radio

[
[edit](/w/index.php?title=Pittsburgh&action=edit&section=48 "Edit section: Radio")

]

A wide variety of
[radio stations](/wiki/Radio_broadcasting "Radio broadcasting")
serve the Pittsburgh market. The first was
[KDKA](/wiki/KDKA_(AM) "KDKA (AM)")
1020 AM, also the world's first commercially licensed radio station, which began airing on November 2, 1920.
[[
244
]](#cite_note-KDKA_AM_1020-254)
Other stations include
[KQV](/wiki/KQV "KQV")
1410 AM (
[news](/wiki/News_radio "News radio")
),
[WBGG](/wiki/WBGG_(AM) "WBGG (AM)")
970 AM (
[sports](/wiki/Sports_radio "Sports radio")
),
[KDKA-FM](/wiki/KDKA-FM "KDKA-FM")
93.7 FM (sports),
[WKST-FM](/wiki/WKST-FM "WKST-FM")
96.1 FM (
[Top 40](/wiki/Mainstream_Top_40 "Mainstream Top 40")
),
[WAMO-AM](/wiki/WAMO-AM "WAMO-AM")
660 AM and 107.3 FM (
[urban contemporary](/wiki/Urban_contemporary_music "Urban contemporary music")
)
[WBZZ](/wiki/WBZZ "WBZZ")
100.7 FM (
[adult contemporary](/wiki/Adult_contemporary_music "Adult contemporary music")
),
[WDVE](/wiki/WDVE "WDVE")
102.5 FM (
[album rock](/wiki/Album_Rock "Album Rock")
),
[WPGB](/wiki/WPGB "WPGB")
104.7 FM (Country), and
[WXDX](/wiki/WXDX "WXDX")
105.9 FM (
[modern rock](/wiki/Modern_rock "Modern rock")
). There are also three
[public radio stations](/wiki/Public_broadcasting "Public broadcasting")
in the area:
[WESA](/wiki/WESA_(FM) "WESA (FM)")
90.5 FM (
[National Public Radio](/wiki/National_Public_Radio "National Public Radio")
affiliate),
[WQED](/wiki/WQED-FM "WQED-FM")
89.3 FM (classical), and
[WYEP](/wiki/WYEP "WYEP")
91.3 FM (
[adult alternative](/wiki/Adult_Alternative "Adult Alternative")
). Three non-commercial stations are run by
[Carnegie Mellon University](/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University "Carnegie Mellon University")
(
[WRCT](/wiki/WRCT "WRCT")
88.3 FM), the
[University of Pittsburgh](/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh "University of Pittsburgh")
(
[WPTS](/wiki/WPTS "WPTS")
92.1 FM), and
[Point Park University](/wiki/Point_Park_University "Point Park University")
(
[WPPJ](/wiki/WPPJ "WPPJ")
670 AM).

### Film

[
[edit](/w/index.php?title=Pittsburgh&action=edit&section=49 "Edit section: Film")

]

Pittsburgh's 116-year-old film industry accelerated after the 2006 passage of the
[Pennsylvania Film Production Tax Credit](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Film_Production_Tax_Credit "Pennsylvania Film Production Tax Credit")
.
[[
245
]](#cite_note-255)
According to the
[Pittsburgh Film Office](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Film_Office "Pittsburgh Film Office")
, over 124 major motion pictures have been filmed, in whole or in part, in Pittsburgh, including
*[The Mothman Prophecies](/wiki/The_Mothman_Prophecies "The Mothman Prophecies")*
,
*[Wonder Boys](/wiki/Wonder_Boys_(film) "Wonder Boys (film)")*
,
[[
246
]](#cite_note-post-gazette.com-256)
*[Dogma](/wiki/Dogma_(film) "Dogma (film)")*
,
[[
246
]](#cite_note-post-gazette.com-256)
*[Hoffa](/wiki/Hoffa_(film) "Hoffa (film)")*
,
*[The Silence of the Lambs](/wiki/The_Silence_of_the_Lambs_(film) "The Silence of the Lambs (film)")*
,
[[
246
]](#cite_note-post-gazette.com-256)
*[Sudden Death](/wiki/Sudden_Death_(1995_film) "Sudden Death (1995 film)")*
,
*[Flashdance](/wiki/Flashdance "Flashdance")*
,
[[
246
]](#cite_note-post-gazette.com-256)
[*Southpaw*](/wiki/Southpaw_(film) "Southpaw (film)")
,
*[Striking Distance](/wiki/Striking_Distance "Striking Distance")*
,
*[Mrs. Soffel](/wiki/Mrs._Soffel "Mrs. Soffel")*
,
[*Jack Reacher*](/wiki/Jack_Reacher_(film) "Jack Reacher (film)")
,
*[Inspector Gadget](/wiki/Inspector_Gadget_(film) "Inspector Gadget (film)")*
,
*[The Next Three Days](/wiki/The_Next_Three_Days "The Next Three Days")*
,
*[The Perks of Being a Wallflower](/wiki/The_Perks_of_Being_a_Wallflower "The Perks of Being a Wallflower")*
,
[[
246
]](#cite_note-post-gazette.com-256)
*[Zack and Miri Make a Porno](/wiki/Zack_and_Miri_Make_a_Porno "Zack and Miri Make a Porno")*
, and
*[Fences](/wiki/Fences_(film) "Fences (film)")*
.
[[
246
]](#cite_note-post-gazette.com-256)
[[
247
]](#cite_note-257)
Pittsburgh became "Gotham City" in 2011 during filming of
*[The Dark Knight Rises](/wiki/The_Dark_Knight_Rises "The Dark Knight Rises")*
.
[[
147
]](#cite_note-Film-152)
[George A. Romero](/wiki/George_A._Romero "George A. Romero")
shot nearly all his films in the area, including his
*[Living Dead](/wiki/Romero%27s_Dead_series "Romero's Dead series")*
series.
[[
248
]](#cite_note-258)
From 2017 to 2023, Pittsburgh welcomed a series of major film and television productions like
*[Fences](/wiki/Fences_(film) "Fences (film)")*
,
*[Mindhunter](/wiki/Mindhunter_(TV_series) "Mindhunter (TV series)")*
,
*[Ma Rainey's Black Bottom](/wiki/Ma_Rainey%27s_Black_Bottom "Ma Rainey's Black Bottom")*
,
*[Sweet Girl](/wiki/Sweet_Girl_(film) "Sweet Girl (film)")*
, and
*[I'm Your Woman](/wiki/I%27m_Your_Woman_(film) "I'm Your Woman (film)")*
, significantly contributing to the local economy.
[[
249
]](#cite_note-259)
[[
250
]](#cite_note-260)

Film production in Pittsburgh has notably impacted the region's economy and job creation, largely due to the 25% tax credit incentive established in 2007.
[[
251
]](#cite_note-261)
[[
252
]](#cite_note-Film_Pittsburgh-262)
The Pittsburgh Film Office states that the film and television industry provides employment to over 10,000 people and pays over $500 million in wages in southwestern Pennsylvania.
[[
253
]](#cite_note-263)
Furthermore, the industry supports over 345,000 local businesses and contributes over $41 billion to them.
[[
252
]](#cite_note-Film_Pittsburgh-262)

Pittsburgh is home to several film festivals, film schools, and organizations that encourage and promote independent and diverse filmmakers. Notable film festivals include the
[Three Rivers Film Festival](/wiki/Three_Rivers_Film_Festival "Three Rivers Film Festival")
, the Pittsburgh Shorts Film Festival, the JFilm Festival, the ReelAbilities Film Festival, and the Black Bottom Film Festival.
[[
254
]](#cite_note-264)
[[
255
]](#cite_note-265)
The local film schools include
[Pittsburgh Filmmakers](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Filmmakers "Pittsburgh Filmmakers")
,
[Point Park University](/wiki/Point_Park_University "Point Park University")
- Cinema & Digital Arts, and
[University of Pittsburgh](/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh "University of Pittsburgh")
- Film Studies.
[[
256
]](#cite_note-266)
[[
257
]](#cite_note-267)

Moreover, Pittsburgh is developing a robust film studio infrastructure, with several sound stages and production facilities available for hire. Prominent film studios in Pittsburgh are 3 Rivers Studios, Cinelease Studios, Post Script Films, Deeplocal, and The Videohouse.
[[
258
]](#cite_note-268)
[[
259
]](#cite_note-269)
[[
260
]](#cite_note-270)
[[
261
]](#cite_note-271)
[[
262
]](#cite_note-272)
There are also plans in the pipeline to develop a new film studio complex at the
[Carrie Furnace](/wiki/Carrie_Furnace "Carrie Furnace")
site in
[Rankin](/wiki/Rankin,_Pennsylvania "Rankin, Pennsylvania")
and
[Swissvale](/wiki/Swissvale "Swissvale")
.
[[
263
]](#cite_note-273)

## Utilities

[
[edit](/w/index.php?title=Pittsburgh&action=edit&section=50 "Edit section: Utilities")

]

Further information:
[Allegheny County Sanitary Authority](/wiki/Allegheny_County_Sanitary_Authority "Allegheny County Sanitary Authority")

The city is served by
[Duquesne Light](/wiki/Duquesne_Light "Duquesne Light")
, one of the original 1912 power companies founded by
[George Westinghouse](/wiki/George_Westinghouse "George Westinghouse")
.
[[
264
]](#cite_note-274)
Water service is provided by the
[Pittsburgh Water and Sewer Authority](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Water_and_Sewer_Authority "Pittsburgh Water and Sewer Authority")
[[
265
]](#cite_note-PWSA-275)
and
[Pennsylvania American Water](/w/index.php?title=Pennsylvania_American_Water&action=edit&redlink=1 "Pennsylvania American Water (page does not exist)")
. Natural gas is provided by
[Equitable Gas](/wiki/Equitable_Gas "Equitable Gas")
,
[Columbia Gas](/wiki/Columbia_Gas "Columbia Gas")
,
[Dominion Resources](/wiki/Dominion_Resources "Dominion Resources")
,
[Direct Energy](/wiki/Direct_Energy "Direct Energy")
, and Novec.
[[
266
]](#cite_note-276)

## Health care

[
[edit](/w/index.php?title=Pittsburgh&action=edit&section=51 "Edit section: Health care")

]

See also:
[List of hospitals in Pittsburgh](/wiki/List_of_hospitals_in_Pittsburgh "List of hospitals in Pittsburgh")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/UPMC_Presbyterian_Hospital_from_Schenley_Park%2C_2015-10-01%2C_01.jpg/220px-UPMC_Presbyterian_Hospital_from_Schenley_Park%2C_2015-10-01%2C_01.jpg)](/wiki/File:UPMC_Presbyterian_Hospital_from_Schenley_Park,_2015-10-01,_01.jpg)


[UPMC](/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh_Medical_Center "University of Pittsburgh Medical Center")
's flagship,
[UPMC Presbyterian](/wiki/UPMC_Presbyterian "UPMC Presbyterian")


[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Allegheny_General_Hospital_2017.jpg/220px-Allegheny_General_Hospital_2017.jpg)](/wiki/File:Allegheny_General_Hospital_2017.jpg)


[Allegheny General Hospital](/wiki/Allegheny_General_Hospital "Allegheny General Hospital")
, the flagship of the
[Allegheny Health Network](/wiki/Allegheny_Health_Network "Allegheny Health Network")

The two largest area health care providers are the
[University of Pittsburgh Medical Center](/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh_Medical_Center "University of Pittsburgh Medical Center")
(UPMC) (since 1893) and
[Allegheny Health Network](/wiki/Allegheny_Health_Network "Allegheny Health Network")
(since 1882). Both hospitals annually rank as among the best overall in the United States, with UPMC ranked among
*[U.S. News & World Report](/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report "U.S. News & World Report")*

's
"Honor Roll" every year since 2000.
[
*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*
]

The first military hospital in U.S. history and the first west of the Atlantic Plain—General Edward Hand Hospital—served the area from 1777 to 1845.
[[
267
]](#cite_note-277)
Since 1847, Pittsburgh has hosted the world's first "Mercy Hospital".
[[
268
]](#cite_note-278)
This was followed by West Penn hospital in 1848, Passavant Hospital in 1849,
[[
26
]](#cite_note-chronology-26)
the
[University of Pittsburgh School of Medicine](/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh_School_of_Medicine "University of Pittsburgh School of Medicine")
in 1883, Children's Hospital in 1887, and
[Magee Womens Hospital](/wiki/Magee-Womens_Hospital_of_UPMC "Magee-Womens Hospital of UPMC")
in 1911. In 1954, Allegheny General (AGH) was among the first to administer
[Cobalt therapy](/wiki/Cobalt_therapy "Cobalt therapy")
.
[[
269
]](#cite_note-279)

In 1980, UPMC announced a $250 million ($1.08 billion today) expansion and also hired transplant pioneer
[Thomas Starzl](/wiki/Thomas_Starzl "Thomas Starzl")
.
[[
270
]](#cite_note-280)
In 1984, Allegheny General surgeons pioneered modern brain surgery. Starzl arranged the 1985 liver transplant of 5-year-old Amie Garrison as a UPMC surgery team flew to
[Baylor University](/wiki/Baylor_University "Baylor University")
, starting its transplant program.
[[
271
]](#cite_note-281)
Also in 1985, UPMC surgeons Drs. Griffith, Hardesty, and Trento revealed a new device after a heart-lung transplant. In 1986, UPMC announced a $230 million ($660 million today) modernization. In 1996, UPMC's planned Sicily
[ISMETT](/wiki/ISMETT "ISMETT")
branch was approved by the Italian government as transplant surgeons to supervise and deliver the world's third (both earlier ones done at UPMC)--and first public—cross species marrow transplant at
[University of California, San Francisco](/wiki/University_of_California,_San_Francisco "University of California, San Francisco")
.
[[
272
]](#cite_note-282)
UPMC's Thomas Detre founded the
[International Society for Bipolar Disorders](/wiki/International_Society_for_Bipolar_Disorders "International Society for Bipolar Disorders")
at a world medical conference in Pittsburgh in 1999.
[[
273
]](#cite_note-283)

The $80 million ($146 million today)
[UPMC Sports Performance Complex](/wiki/UPMC_Sports_Performance_Complex "UPMC Sports Performance Complex")
for the
[Pittsburgh Panthers](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Panthers "Pittsburgh Panthers")
&
[Pittsburgh Steelers](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Steelers "Pittsburgh Steelers")
opened in 2000. In 2002, AGH opened its $30 million ($53.3 million today), 5-floor, 100,000 sq. ft., cancer center. The $130 million ($227 million today) 350,000 sq. ft.
[Hillman Cancer Center](/wiki/Hillman_Cancer_Center "Hillman Cancer Center")
opened in 2003 as UPMC entered into an 8-year, $420 million ($699 million today) agreement with
[IBM](/wiki/IBM "IBM")
to upgrade medical technologies & health information systems.
[
*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*
]

In 2009, the $600 million ($876 million today)
[UPMC Children's Hospital of Pittsburgh](/wiki/UPMC_Children%27s_Hospital_of_Pittsburgh "UPMC Children's Hospital of Pittsburgh")
opened. The campus was featured in world news in 2012 for several unique approaches to patient care.
[[
274
]](#cite_note-284)
UPMC officially adopted in
[Erie, Pennsylvania](/wiki/Erie,_Pennsylvania "Erie, Pennsylvania")
's
[Hamot Medical Center](/wiki/UPMC_Hamot "UPMC Hamot")
in 2010. The
[Pittsburgh Penguins](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Penguins "Pittsburgh Penguins")
announced a state of the art training facility with UPMC in 2012.
[[
275
]](#cite_note-285)
UPMC announced in 2013 it had partnered with
[Nazarbayev University](/wiki/Nazarbayev_University "Nazarbayev University")
to help found its medical school.
[[
276
]](#cite_note-286)

### Health discoveries

[
[edit](/w/index.php?title=Pittsburgh&action=edit&section=52 "Edit section: Health discoveries")

]

While he was a professor at the
[University of Pittsburgh](/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh "University of Pittsburgh")
, American virologist
[Jonas Salk](/wiki/Jonas_Salk "Jonas Salk")
developed one of the first successful
[polio vaccines](/wiki/Polio_vaccine "Polio vaccine")
, which came into use in 1955.

UPMC has pioneered several world firsts including the first known cystic fibrosis heart-lung transplant (1983), the world's first simultaneous liver and heart transplant operation on a child (6-year-old
[Stormie Jones](/wiki/Stormie_Jones "Stormie Jones")
in 1984), the youngest heart-lung transplant (9 years old in 1985), the world's first heart-liver-kidney transplant (1989), the world's first heart-liver transplant on an infant (1997),
[[
277
]](#cite_note-287)
the first pediatric heart-double lung-liver transplant (1998), the nation's first double hand transplant (2009), and the first total forearm and hand transplant (2010), as well as the state's first heart transplant (1968).
[[
278
]](#cite_note-288)
[[
279
]](#cite_note-289)

*[The Lancet](/wiki/The_Lancet "The Lancet")*
published a 2012 UPMC study of two 9-year quadriplegics being able to move a robotic arm by thought, to pick up objects, shake hands, and even eat. Wiring the brain around spine damage to restore arm and leg muscle function was successful using robotic arms controlled via an embedded computer to translate signals near a small group of neurons with 200 needles.
[[
280
]](#cite_note-290)

## Transportation

[
[edit](/w/index.php?title=Pittsburgh&action=edit&section=53 "Edit section: Transportation")

]

Main article:
[Transportation in Pittsburgh](/wiki/Transportation_in_Pittsburgh "Transportation in Pittsburgh")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Pittsburgh_-_Acrisure_Stadium_and_Skyline_%2853910936115%29.jpg/220px-Pittsburgh_-_Acrisure_Stadium_and_Skyline_%2853910936115%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Pittsburgh_-_Acrisure_Stadium_and_Skyline_(53910936115).jpg)


Aerial view of some of Pittsburgh's numerous bridges

Pittsburgh is a
[city of bridges](/wiki/Bridges_of_Pittsburgh "Bridges of Pittsburgh")
. With 446,
[[
281
]](#cite_note-venice-291)
it has three bridges more than
[Venice](/wiki/Venice "Venice")
, Italy, which has historically held the title "City of Bridges".
[[
282
]](#cite_note-292)
Around 40 bridges cross the three rivers near the city. The
[Smithfield Street Bridge](/wiki/Smithfield_Street_Bridge "Smithfield Street Bridge")
was the world's first lenticular truss bridge. The city's
[Three Sisters Bridges](/wiki/Three_Sisters_Bridges "Three Sisters Bridges")
offer a picturesque view of the city from the North. The southwestern "entrance" to Downtown for travelers coming in from
[Interstate 79](/wiki/Interstate_79 "Interstate 79")
and the
[Pittsburgh International Airport](/wiki/Pittsburgh_International_Airport "Pittsburgh International Airport")
is through the
[Fort Pitt Tunnel](/wiki/Fort_Pitt_Tunnel "Fort Pitt Tunnel")
and over the
[Fort Pitt Bridge](/wiki/Fort_Pitt_Bridge "Fort Pitt Bridge")
. The
[Fort Duquesne Bridge](/wiki/Fort_Duquesne_Bridge "Fort Duquesne Bridge")
carrying
[Interstate 279](/wiki/Interstate_279 "Interstate 279")
is the main gateway from Downtown to both
[PNC Park](/wiki/PNC_Park "PNC Park")
,
[Acrisure Stadium](/wiki/Acrisure_Stadium "Acrisure Stadium")
and the
[Rivers Casino](/wiki/Rivers_Casino_(Pittsburgh) "Rivers Casino (Pittsburgh)")
. The
[Panhandle Bridge](/wiki/Panhandle_Bridge "Panhandle Bridge")
carries Pittsburgh Regional Transit's Blue/Red/Silver subway lines across the
[Monongahela River](/wiki/Monongahela_River "Monongahela River")
. The renovated
[J&L Steel Company](/wiki/Jones_and_Laughlin_Steel_Company "Jones and Laughlin Steel Company")
bridge has been a key traffic/running-biking trail conduit connecting the
[Southside Works](/wiki/Southside_Works "Southside Works")
and
[Pittsburgh Technology Center](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Technology_Center "Pittsburgh Technology Center")
. Over 2,000 bridges span the landscape of Allegheny County.
[[
283
]](#cite_note-293)

### Public transportation statistics

[
[edit](/w/index.php?title=Pittsburgh&action=edit&section=54 "Edit section: Public transportation statistics")

]

Pittsburgh is served by
[Pittsburgh Regional Transit](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Regional_Transit "Pittsburgh Regional Transit")
, the 26th-largest transit agency in the country prior to the
[COVID-19 pandemic](/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic_in_the_United_States "COVID-19 pandemic in the United States")
. The average amount of time people spend commuting with public transit in Pittsburgh, for example to and from work, on a weekday is 73 minutes, while 23% of public transit riders ride for more than two hours every day. The average amount of time people wait at a stop or station for public transit is 17 minutes, while 33% of riders wait for over 20 minutes on average every day. The average distance people usually ride in a single trip with public transit is 3.9 mi (6.3 km), while 11% travel for over 7.5 mi (12 km) in a single direction.
[[
284
]](#cite_note-294)

### Expressways and highways

[
[edit](/w/index.php?title=Pittsburgh&action=edit&section=55 "Edit section: Expressways and highways")

]

| Expressways | | Other Highways | | |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  | [Parkway North](/wiki/Interstate_279 "Interstate 279") |  | [US 19](/wiki/U.S._Route_19_in_Pennsylvania "U.S. Route 19 in Pennsylvania") | [PA 88](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_88 "Pennsylvania Route 88") |
|  | [Parkway East & West](/wiki/Interstate_376 "Interstate 376") |  | [Truck   US 19](/wiki/U.S._Route_19_Truck_(Pittsburgh,_Pennsylvania) "U.S. Route 19 Truck (Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania)") | [PA 121](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_121 "Pennsylvania Route 121") |
|  | [Crosstown](/wiki/Interstate_579 "Interstate 579") |  | [PA 8](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_8 "Pennsylvania Route 8") | [PA 130](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_130 "Pennsylvania Route 130") |
|  | [Allegheny Valley Expressway](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_28 "Pennsylvania Route 28") |  | [PA 50](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_50 "Pennsylvania Route 50") | [PA 380](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_380 "Pennsylvania Route 380") |
|  | [Ohio River Boulevard](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_65 "Pennsylvania Route 65") |  | [PA 51](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_51 "Pennsylvania Route 51") | [PA 837](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_837 "Pennsylvania Route 837") |
|  |  |  | [PA 60](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_60 "Pennsylvania Route 60") | [PA 885](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_885 "Pennsylvania Route 885") |

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Pittsburgh_%28view_from_the_North_Hills%29.JPG/220px-Pittsburgh_%28view_from_the_North_Hills%29.JPG)](/wiki/File:Pittsburgh_(view_from_the_North_Hills).JPG)


[I-279](/wiki/Interstate_279 "Interstate 279")

Locals refer to the interstates fanning out from
[downtown Pittsburgh](/wiki/Downtown_Pittsburgh "Downtown Pittsburgh")
as the "parkways."
[Interstate 376](/wiki/Interstate_376 "Interstate 376")
is both the "parkway east" connecting to
[Interstate 76](/wiki/Interstate_76_(Ohio%E2%80%93New_Jersey) "Interstate 76 (Ohio–New Jersey)")
(
[Pennsylvania Turnpike](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Turnpike "Pennsylvania Turnpike")
) and the "parkway west" connecting to
[Interstate 79](/wiki/Interstate_79 "Interstate 79")
, the
[Pittsburgh International Airport](/wiki/Pittsburgh_International_Airport "Pittsburgh International Airport")
, the Ohio end of the Turnpike and
[Interstate 80](/wiki/Interstate_80 "Interstate 80")
. The "parkway north" is
[Interstate 279](/wiki/Interstate_279 "Interstate 279")
connecting to I-79. The "crosstown" is
[Interstate 579](/wiki/Interstate_579 "Interstate 579")
allowing access to the heart of downtown, the
[Liberty Tunnels](/wiki/Liberty_Tunnels "Liberty Tunnels")
and the
[PPG Paints Arena](/wiki/PPG_Paints_Arena "PPG Paints Arena")
. The 45-mile-long and 70-mile-long expressway sections of
[Pennsylvania Route 28](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_28 "Pennsylvania Route 28")
and
[U.S. Route 22](/wiki/U.S._Route_22 "U.S. Route 22")
also carry traffic from downtown to the northeast and western suburbs, respectively.
[Interstate 70](/wiki/Interstate_70 "Interstate 70")
, 79 and 76 (the Turnpike) roughly form a triangular-shaped "beltway" with
[Interstate 68](/wiki/Interstate_68 "Interstate 68")
and 80 within the media market's northern and southern limits. Turnpike spurs such as the
[Mon–Fayette Expressway](/wiki/Mon%E2%80%93Fayette_Expressway "Mon–Fayette Expressway")
,
[Pennsylvania Route 576](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_576 "Pennsylvania Route 576")
and
[Route 66](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_66#Amos_K._Hutchinson_Bypass "Pennsylvania Route 66")
also help traffic flow. The non-expressway
[Pittsburgh/Allegheny County Belt System](/wiki/Pittsburgh/Allegheny_County_Belt_System "Pittsburgh/Allegheny County Belt System")
serves navigation in the region.

### Airports

[
[edit](/w/index.php?title=Pittsburgh&action=edit&section=56 "Edit section: Airports")

]

Main article:
[Pittsburgh metropolitan area § Airports](/wiki/Pittsburgh_metropolitan_area#Airports "Pittsburgh metropolitan area")

[Pittsburgh International Airport](/wiki/Pittsburgh_International_Airport "Pittsburgh International Airport")
provides commercial passenger service from over 15 airlines to the
[Pittsburgh metropolitan area](/wiki/Pittsburgh_metropolitan_area "Pittsburgh metropolitan area")
.
[Arnold Palmer Regional Airport](/wiki/Arnold_Palmer_Regional_Airport "Arnold Palmer Regional Airport")
also provides limited commercial passenger service and is 44 miles (71 km) east of Pittsburgh.

Other airports that have or have had scheduled commercial service include
[Morgantown Municipal Airport](/wiki/Morgantown_Municipal_Airport "Morgantown Municipal Airport")
(79 miles (127 km) south of Pittsburgh),
[Youngstown–Warren Regional Airport](/wiki/Youngstown%E2%80%93Warren_Regional_Airport "Youngstown–Warren Regional Airport")
(81 miles (130 km) northwest of Pittsburgh),
[Akron–Canton Airport](/wiki/Akron%E2%80%93Canton_Airport "Akron–Canton Airport")
(120 miles (190 km) northwest of Pittsburgh),
[Johnstown–Cambria County Airport](/wiki/Johnstown%E2%80%93Cambria_County_Airport "Johnstown–Cambria County Airport")
(60 miles (97 km) east of Pittsburgh) and
[Erie International Airport](/wiki/Erie_International_Airport "Erie International Airport")
(123 miles (198 km) north of Pittsburgh).

### Intercity passenger rail and bus

[
[edit](/w/index.php?title=Pittsburgh&action=edit&section=57 "Edit section: Intercity passenger rail and bus")

]

Main articles:
[Union Station (Pittsburgh)](/wiki/Union_Station_(Pittsburgh) "Union Station (Pittsburgh)")
and
[Grant Street Transportation Center](/wiki/Grant_Street_Transportation_Center "Grant Street Transportation Center")

[Amtrak](/wiki/Amtrak "Amtrak")
provides intercity rail service to
[Pittsburgh Union Station](/wiki/Union_Station_(Pittsburgh) "Union Station (Pittsburgh)")
, via the
*[Capitol Limited](/wiki/Capitol_Limited_(Amtrak_train) "Capitol Limited (Amtrak train)")*
between Chicago and Washington, D.C., and the
*[Pennsylvanian](/wiki/Pennsylvanian_(train) "Pennsylvanian (train)")*
to New York City.

[Megabus](/wiki/Megabus_(North_America) "Megabus (North America)")
,
[Greyhound Lines](/wiki/Greyhound_Lines "Greyhound Lines")
, and
[Fullington Trailways](/wiki/Fullington_Trailways "Fullington Trailways")
connect Pittsburgh with distant cities by bus; Greyhound and Fullington Trailways buses stop at the
[Grant Street Transportation Center](/wiki/Grant_Street_Transportation_Center "Grant Street Transportation Center")
intercity bus terminal. Popular destinations include
[Philadelphia](/wiki/Philadelphia "Philadelphia")
,
[New York City](/wiki/New_York_City "New York City")
, and
[Washington, D.C.](/wiki/Washington,_D.C. "Washington, D.C.")
[[
285
]](#cite_note-295)

Until declines in passenger travel in the 1950s and 1960s, several stations served Pittsburgh:
[Baltimore & Ohio Station](/wiki/Baltimore_and_Ohio_Station_(Pittsburgh) "Baltimore and Ohio Station (Pittsburgh)")
,
[Pittsburgh & Lake Erie Railroad Station](/wiki/Pittsburgh_%26_Lake_Erie_Railroad_Station "Pittsburgh & Lake Erie Railroad Station")
,
[Wabash Pittsburgh Terminal](/wiki/Wabash_Pittsburgh_Terminal "Wabash Pittsburgh Terminal")
and Pittsburgh Union Station.

### Regional mass transit

[
[edit](/w/index.php?title=Pittsburgh&action=edit&section=58 "Edit section: Regional mass transit")

]

Main articles:
[Pittsburgh Light Rail](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Light_Rail "Pittsburgh Light Rail")
and
[Port Authority of Allegheny County § Bus rapid transit](/wiki/Port_Authority_of_Allegheny_County#Bus_rapid_transit "Port Authority of Allegheny County")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Steel_Plaza_Subway_Station.JPG/220px-Steel_Plaza_Subway_Station.JPG)](/wiki/File:Steel_Plaza_Subway_Station.JPG)


[Steel Plaza subway station](/wiki/Steel_Plaza_(PAT_station) "Steel Plaza (PAT station)")

[Pittsburgh Regional Transit](/wiki/Port_Authority_of_Allegheny_County "Port Authority of Allegheny County")
, formerly known as the Port Authority of Allegheny County, is the region's mass transit system. While serving only a portion of the Pittsburgh area, the nation's 20th-largest metropolitan area, it is the 11th-largest transit agency in the United States.
[[
286
]](#cite_note-296)
Pittsburgh Regional Transit runs a network of intracity and intercity bus routes, the
[Monongahela Incline](/wiki/Monongahela_Incline "Monongahela Incline")
[Funicular](/wiki/Funicular "Funicular")
railway (more commonly known as an "incline") on Mount Washington, a
[light rail](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Light_Rail "Pittsburgh Light Rail")
system that runs mostly above-ground in the suburbs and underground as a subway in the city, and one of the nation's largest
[busway](/wiki/Bus_rapid_transit "Bus rapid transit")
systems.
[[
287
]](#cite_note-297)
Pittsburgh Regional Transit owns the
[Duquesne Incline](/wiki/Duquesne_Incline "Duquesne Incline")
but it is operated by a non-profit preservation trust,
[[
288
]](#cite_note-298)
but accepts Pittsburgh Regional Transit passes and charges PRT fares.

The Bus System lines are
[labeled by number and letter](/wiki/List_of_bus_routes_in_Pittsburgh "List of bus routes in Pittsburgh")
. These are the largest portion of Pittsburgh Regional Transit and serve on streets and designated busways. Buses serve most of the county, extending as far as
[Pittsburgh International Airport](/wiki/Pittsburgh_International_Airport "Pittsburgh International Airport")
,
[Monroeville](/wiki/Monroeville,_Pennsylvania "Monroeville, Pennsylvania")
,
[McCandless](/wiki/McCandless,_Pennsylvania "McCandless, Pennsylvania")
, and the borders of
[Westmoreland County](/wiki/Westmoreland_County,_Pennsylvania "Westmoreland County, Pennsylvania")
and
[Beaver County, Pennsylvania](/wiki/Beaver_County,_Pennsylvania "Beaver County, Pennsylvania")
. Meanwhile,
[the light rail system](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Light_Rail "Pittsburgh Light Rail")
(commonly known as the "T") runs along both new tracks and those refurbished from the streetcar era. The light rail runs from
[Acrisure Stadium](/wiki/Acrisure_Stadium "Acrisure Stadium")
to
[South Hills Village](/wiki/South_Hills_Village "South Hills Village")
and
[Library](/wiki/Library,_Pennsylvania "Library, Pennsylvania")
, taking commuters through one of two routes: one which serves
[Castle Shannon](/wiki/Castle_Shannon,_Pennsylvania "Castle Shannon, Pennsylvania")
,
[Mt. Lebanon](/wiki/Mt._Lebanon,_Pennsylvania "Mt. Lebanon, Pennsylvania")
, and
[Beechview](/wiki/Beechview_(Pittsburgh) "Beechview (Pittsburgh)")
, and the other is an express line using railways through
[Overbrook](/wiki/Overbrook_(Pittsburgh) "Overbrook (Pittsburgh)")
.

### Freight rail

[
[edit](/w/index.php?title=Pittsburgh&action=edit&section=59 "Edit section: Freight rail")

]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Pittsburgh_Penn_Station_%286962200876%29.jpg/220px-Pittsburgh_Penn_Station_%286962200876%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Pittsburgh_Penn_Station_(6962200876).jpg)


[Union Station](/wiki/Union_Station_(Pittsburgh) "Union Station (Pittsburgh)")
, built in 1903

Pittsburgh's rail industry dates to 1851 when the
[Pennsylvania Railroad](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Railroad "Pennsylvania Railroad")
first opened service between the Pittsburgh and
[Philadelphia](/wiki/Philadelphia "Philadelphia")
. The
[Baltimore and Ohio Railroad](/wiki/Baltimore_and_Ohio_Railroad "Baltimore and Ohio Railroad")
entered the city in 1871. In 1865,
[Andrew Carnegie](/wiki/Andrew_Carnegie "Andrew Carnegie")
opened the
[Pittsburgh Locomotive and Car Works](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Locomotive_and_Car_Works "Pittsburgh Locomotive and Car Works")
, which manufactured for the industry until 1919. Carnegie also founded the
[Union Railroad](/wiki/Union_Railroad_(Pittsburgh) "Union Railroad (Pittsburgh)")
in 1894 for heavy freight services and it still serves the area's steel industry, while
[George Westinghouse](/wiki/George_Westinghouse "George Westinghouse")
's
[Wabtec](/wiki/Wabtec "Wabtec")
has been a leader in rail engines and switching since 1869.

Pittsburgh is home to one of
[Norfolk Southern Railway](/wiki/Norfolk_Southern_Railway "Norfolk Southern Railway")
's busiest freight corridors, the
[Pittsburgh Line](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Line "Pittsburgh Line")
, and operates up to 70 trains per day through the city. The suburban
[Conway Rail Yard](/wiki/Conway_Yard "Conway Yard")
, built in 1889, was the largest freight rail center in the world from 1956 until 1980 and is today the nation's second-largest.
[CSX](/wiki/CSX "CSX")
, the other major freight railroad in the eastern U.S., also has
[major operations](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Subdivision "Pittsburgh Subdivision")
around Pittsburgh.

### Port

[
[edit](/w/index.php?title=Pittsburgh&action=edit&section=60 "Edit section: Port")

]

The
[Port of Pittsburgh](/wiki/Port_of_Pittsburgh_Commission "Port of Pittsburgh Commission")
ranks as the
[20th-largest port](/wiki/List_of_ports_in_the_United_States "List of ports in the United States")
in the United States with almost 34 million short tons of river cargo for 2011. The port ranked ninth-largest in the U.S. when measured in domestic trade.
[[
289
]](#cite_note-299)

   ,

   ,

   ,

   ,

2. **[^](#cite_ref-76)**

   The warmest daily minimum at the current observation location, Pittsburgh Int'l, is only 77 °F (25 °C) on July 23, 2010, and July 16, 1980.
   [[
   74
   ]](#cite_note-NOAA-75)
3. **[^](#cite_ref-80)**

   Mean monthly maxima and minima (i.e. the highest and lowest temperature readings during an entire month or year) calculated based on data at said location from 1991 to 2020.
4. **[^](#cite_ref-81)**

   Records kept September 1874 to June 1935 at the Weather Bureau Office across the Allegheny River from
   [downtown](/wiki/Downtown_Pittsburgh "Downtown Pittsburgh")
   , at
   [Allegheny County Airport](/wiki/Allegheny_County_Airport "Allegheny County Airport")
   from July 1935 to 14 September 1952, and at Pittsburgh Int'l (KPIT) since 15 September 1952. Due to its river valley and urban location as well as elevation, many of the summertime warm minima temperature records set at the WBO have not even come close to being matched at KPIT, which is at-elevation and located in the western suburbs. For more information, see
   [Threadex](http://threadex.rcc-acis.org)
5. ^
   [***a***](#cite_ref-fifteen_114-0)
   [***b***](#cite_ref-fifteen_114-1)

   From 15% sample
6. **[^](#cite_ref-205)**

   The
   [Pittsburgh Power](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Power "Pittsburgh Power")
   of the
   [Arena Football League](/wiki/Arena_Football_League_(2010%E2%80%93) "Arena Football League (2010–)")
   and the
   [Pittsburgh Passion](/wiki/Pittsburgh_Passion "Pittsburgh Passion")
   of the
   [Independent Women's Football League](/wiki/Independent_Women%27s_Football_League "Independent Women's Football League")
   (IWFL) use these colors as well.
7. **[^](#cite_ref-301)**

   Pittsburgh and Sheffield are both known as
   [Steel City](/wiki/Steel_City "Steel City")
   for their connections with the steel industry.

## Further reading

[
[edit](/w/index.php?title=Pittsburgh&action=edit&section=66 "Edit section: Further reading")

]

* Allen Dieterich-Ward,
  *Beyond Rust: Metropolitan Pittsburgh and the Fate of Industrial America*
  (U of Pennsylvania Press, 2016). viii, 347 pp.
* Kenneth J. Kobus,
  *City of Steel: How Pittsburgh Became the World's Steelmaking Capital During the Carnegie Era.*
  Lanham, MD: Rowman and Littlefield, 2015.
* Charles McCollester,
  *[The Point of Pittsburgh](/wiki/The_Point_of_Pittsburgh "The Point of Pittsburgh")
  : Production and Struggle at the Forks of the Ohio.*
  Pittsburgh, PA: Battle of Homestead Foundation, 2008.
